<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Radio</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Technology of using radio waves to carry information</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about science and technology. For broadcasting, see <a href="/wiki/Radio_broadcasting" title="Radio broadcasting">Radio broadcasting</a>. For the device, see <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver">Radio receiver</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Radio_(disambiguation)" title="Radio (disambiguation)">Radio (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p><b>Radio</b> is the technology of signaling and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">communicating</a> using <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCMag_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PCMag-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellingson1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellingson1-3">[3]</a></sup> Radio waves are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">electromagnetic waves</a> of <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> between 30 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a> (Hz) and 300 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigahertz" title="Gigahertz">gigahertz</a> (GHz). They are generated by an electronic device called a <a href="/wiki/Transmitter" title="Transmitter">transmitter</a> connected to an <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)" title="Antenna (radio)">antenna</a> which radiates the waves, and received by another antenna connected to a <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver">radio receiver</a>. Radio is very widely used in modern technology, in radio communication, <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radio_navigation" title="Radio navigation">radio navigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Remote_control" title="Remote control">remote control</a>, <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a>, and other applications.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radio_towers_on_Sandia_Peak_-_closeup.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1528" data-file-width="1347" decoding="async" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Radio_towers_on_Sandia_Peak_-_closeup.jpg/290px-Radio_towers_on_Sandia_Peak_-_closeup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Radio_towers_on_Sandia_Peak_-_closeup.jpg/435px-Radio_towers_on_Sandia_Peak_-_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Radio_towers_on_Sandia_Peak_-_closeup.jpg/580px-Radio_towers_on_Sandia_Peak_-_closeup.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radio_towers_on_Sandia_Peak_-_closeup.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A variety of radio <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)" title="Antenna (radio)">antennas</a> on <a href="/wiki/Sandia_Mountains" title="Sandia Mountains">Sandia Peak</a> near <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_New_Mexico" title="Albuquerque, New Mexico">Albuquerque</a>, New Mexico, US</div></div></div>
<p>In <b>radio communication</b>, used in <a href="/wiki/Radio_broadcasting" title="Radio broadcasting">radio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Television_broadcasting" title="Television broadcasting">television broadcasting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_phone" title="Cell phone">cell phones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Two-way_radio" title="Two-way radio">two-way radios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wireless_network" title="Wireless network">wireless networking</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellite_communication" title="Satellite communication">satellite communication</a>, among numerous other uses, radio waves are used to carry information across space from a transmitter to a receiver, by <a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">modulating</a> the radio signal (impressing an information signal on the radio wave by varying some aspect of the wave) in the transmitter. In <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>, used to locate and track objects like aircraft, ships, spacecraft and missiles, a beam of radio waves emitted by a radar transmitter reflects off the target object, and the reflected waves reveal the object's location. In <a href="/wiki/Radio_navigation" title="Radio navigation">radio navigation</a> systems such as <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">GPS</a> and <a href="/wiki/VHF_omnidirectional_range" title="VHF omnidirectional range">VOR</a>, a mobile receiver accepts radio signals from <a href="/wiki/Radio_beacon" title="Radio beacon">navigational radio beacons</a> whose position is known, and by precisely measuring the arrival time of the radio waves the receiver can calculate its position on Earth. In wireless <a href="/wiki/Radio_control" title="Radio control">radio remote control</a> devices like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drone_(aircraft)" title="Drone (aircraft)">drones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garage_door_opener" title="Garage door opener">garage door openers</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keyless_entry_system" title="Keyless entry system">keyless entry systems</a>, radio signals transmitted from a controller device control the actions of a remote device.
</p><p>Applications of radio waves that do not involve transmitting the waves significant distances, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RF_heating" title="RF heating">RF heating</a> used in industrial processes and <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">microwave ovens</a>, and medical uses such as <a href="/wiki/Diathermy" title="Diathermy">diathermy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI_machine" title="MRI machine">MRI machines</a>, are not usually called <i>radio</i>.  The noun <i>radio</i> is also used to mean a <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver">broadcast radio receiver</a>.
</p><p>The existence of radio waves was first proven by German physicist <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">Heinrich Hertz</a> on November 11, 1886.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KIT_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-KIT-4">[4]</a></sup> In the mid 1890s, building on techniques physicists were using to study electromagnetic waves, <a href="/wiki/Guglielmo_Marconi" title="Guglielmo Marconi">Guglielmo Marconi</a> developed the first apparatus for long-distance radio communication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieeexplore.ieee.org_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieeexplore.ieee.org-5">[5]</a></sup> sending a wireless <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morse_Code" title="Morse Code">Morse Code</a> message to a source over a kilometer away in 1895,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1890s_–_1930s:_Radio_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-1890s_–_1930s:_Radio-6">[6]</a></sup> and the first transatlantic signal on December 12, 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.com_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-history.com-7">[7]</a></sup> The first commercial radio broadcast was transmitted on November 2, 1920 when the live returns of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harding-Cox_presidential_election" title="Harding-Cox presidential election">Harding-Cox presidential election</a> were broadcast by Westinghouse Electric and Manufacturing Company in Pittsburgh, under the call sign <a href="/wiki/KDKA_(AM)" title="KDKA (AM)">KDKA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_Commercial_Radio_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Commercial_Radio-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emission of radio waves is regulated by law, coordinated by the <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">International Telecommunication Union</a> (ITU), which allocates frequency bands in the <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a> for different uses.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology">Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Radio waves are radiated by <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charges</a> undergoing <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kraus_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kraus-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serway_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serway-10">[10]</a></sup> They are generated artificially by time varying <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric currents</a>, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> flowing back and forth in a metal conductor called an antenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balanis_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balanis-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellingson2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellingson2-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>As they travel farther from the transmitting antenna, radio waves spread out so their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_strength" title="Signal strength">signal strength</a> (<a href="/wiki/Intensity_(physics)" title="Intensity (physics)">intensity</a> in watts per square meter) decreases, so radio transmissions can only be received within a limited range of the transmitter, the distance depending on the transmitter power, the antenna <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pattern" title="Radiation pattern">radiation pattern</a>, receiver sensitivity, noise level, and presence of obstructions between transmitter and receiver.  An <a href="/wiki/Omnidirectional_antenna" title="Omnidirectional antenna">omnidirectional antenna</a> transmits or receives radio waves in all directions, while a <a href="/wiki/Directional_antenna" title="Directional antenna">directional antenna</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a> transmits radio waves in a beam in a particular direction, or receives waves from only one direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Visser_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Visser-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Radio waves travel at the speed of light in a vacuum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">electromagnetic waves</a> besides radio waves, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">gamma rays</a>, can also carry information and be used for communication.  The wide use of radio waves for telecommunication is mainly due to their desirable <a href="/wiki/Radio_propagation" title="Radio propagation">propagation</a> properties stemming from their large wavelength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellingson2_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellingson2-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_communication">Radio communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Radio communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signal_processing_system.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="387" data-file-width="1204" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Signal_processing_system.png/310px-Signal_processing_system.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Signal_processing_system.png/465px-Signal_processing_system.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Signal_processing_system.png/620px-Signal_processing_system.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signal_processing_system.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Radio communication.  Information such as sound is converted by a transducer such as a <a href="/wiki/Microphone" title="Microphone">microphone</a> to an electrical signal, which modulates a <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio wave</a> produced by the <a href="/wiki/Transmitter" title="Transmitter">transmitter</a>. A receiver intercepts the radio wave and extracts the information-bearing modulation signal, which is converted back to a human usable form with another transducer such as a <a href="/wiki/Loudspeaker" title="Loudspeaker">loudspeaker</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amfm3-en-de.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="256" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Amfm3-en-de.gif/220px-Amfm3-en-de.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Amfm3-en-de.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amfm3-en-de.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of AM and FM modulated radio waves</div></div></div>
<p>In radio communication systems, information is carried across space using radio waves.  At the sending end, the information to be sent is converted by some type of <a href="/wiki/Transducer" title="Transducer">transducer</a> to a time-varying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_signal" title="Electrical signal">electrical signal</a> called the modulation signal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellingson2_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ellingson2-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowStuffWorks_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-HowStuffWorks-18">[18]</a></sup> The modulation signal may be an <a href="/wiki/Audio_signal" title="Audio signal">audio signal</a> representing sound from a <a href="/wiki/Microphone" title="Microphone">microphone</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_signal" title="Video signal">video signal</a> representing moving images from a <a href="/wiki/Video_camera" title="Video camera">video camera</a>, or a <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal" title="Digital signal">digital signal</a> consisting of a sequence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_digit" title="Binary digit">bits</a> representing binary data from a computer.  The modulation signal is applied to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_transmitter" title="Radio transmitter">radio transmitter</a>.  In the transmitter, an <a href="/wiki/Electronic_oscillator" title="Electronic oscillator">electronic oscillator</a> generates an <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">alternating current</a> oscillating at a <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">radio frequency</a>, called the <i><a href="/wiki/Carrier_wave" title="Carrier wave">carrier wave</a></i> because it serves to "carry" the information through the air.  The information signal is used to <a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">modulate</a> the carrier, varying some aspect of the carrier wave, impressing the information on the carrier. Different radio systems use different <a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">modulation</a> methods:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faruque_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faruque-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>AM (<a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">amplitude modulation</a>) – in an AM transmitter, the <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a> (strength) of the radio carrier wave is varied by the modulation signal;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faruque_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faruque-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup></li>
<li>FM (<a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">frequency modulation</a>) – in an FM transmitter, the <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> of the radio carrier wave is varied by the modulation signal;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faruque_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Faruque-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 33">: 33 </span></sup></li>
<li>FSK (<a href="/wiki/Frequency-shift_keying" title="Frequency-shift keying">frequency-shift keying</a>) – used in wireless digital devices to transmit <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal" title="Digital signal">digital signals</a>, the frequency of the carrier wave is shifted between frequencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faruque_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Faruque-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 58">: 58 </span></sup></li>
<li>OFDM (<a href="/wiki/Orthogonal_frequency-division_multiplexing" title="Orthogonal frequency-division multiplexing">orthogonal frequency-division multiplexing</a>) – a family of complicated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_modulation" title="Digital modulation">digital modulation</a> methods very widely used in high bandwidth systems such as <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> networks, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cellphone" title="Cellphone">cellphones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Digital_television" title="Digital television">digital television</a> broadcasting, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_audio_broadcasting" title="Digital audio broadcasting">digital audio broadcasting</a> (DAB) to transmit digital data using a minimum of <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a> bandwidth.  It has higher <a href="/wiki/Spectral_efficiency" title="Spectral efficiency">spectral efficiency</a> and more resistance to <a href="/wiki/Fading" title="Fading">fading</a> than AM or FM.  In OFDM, multiple radio carrier waves closely spaced in frequency are transmitted within the radio channel, with each carrier modulated with bits from the incoming <a href="/wiki/Bitstream" title="Bitstream">bitstream</a> so multiple bits are being sent simultaneously, in parallel.  At the receiver, the carriers are demodulated and the bits are combined in the proper order into one bitstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ergen_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-ergen-20">[20]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Many other types of modulation are also used. In some types, a carrier wave is not transmitted but just one or both modulation <a href="/wiki/Sideband" title="Sideband">sidebands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The modulated carrier is <a href="/wiki/Amplifier" title="Amplifier">amplified</a> in the transmitter and applied to a transmitting <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)" title="Antenna (radio)">antenna</a> which radiates the energy as radio waves.  The radio waves carry the information to the receiver location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the receiver, the radio wave induces a tiny oscillating <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> in the receiving antenna which is a weaker replica of the current in the transmitting antenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellingson2_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ellingson2-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowStuffWorks_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-HowStuffWorks-18">[18]</a></sup> This voltage is applied to the <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver">radio receiver</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Amplifier" title="Amplifier">amplifies</a> the weak radio signal so it is stronger, then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demodulate" title="Demodulate">demodulates</a> it, extracting the original modulation signal from the modulated carrier wave.  The modulation signal is converted by a <a href="/wiki/Transducer" title="Transducer">transducer</a> back to a human-usable form: an audio signal is converted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_wave" title="Sound wave">sound waves</a> by a loudspeaker or earphones, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_signal" title="Video signal">video signal</a> is converted to images by a <a href="/wiki/Display_device" title="Display device">display</a>, while a digital signal is applied to a computer or microprocessor, which interacts with human users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faruque_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-Faruque-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The radio waves from many transmitters pass through the air simultaneously without interfering with each other because each transmitter's radio waves oscillate at a different rate, in other words, each transmitter has a different <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a>, measured in <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a> (Hz), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilohertz" title="Kilohertz">kilohertz</a> (kHz), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megahertz" title="Megahertz">megahertz</a> (MHz) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigahertz" title="Gigahertz">gigahertz</a> (GHz).  The receiving antenna typically picks up the radio signals of many transmitters.  The receiver uses <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuned_circuit" title="Tuned circuit">tuned circuits</a></i> to select the radio signal desired out of all the signals picked up by the antenna and reject the others.  A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuned_circuit" title="Tuned circuit">tuned circuit</a> (also called resonant circuit or tank circuit) acts like a <a href="/wiki/Resonator" title="Resonator">resonator</a>, similarly to a <a href="/wiki/Tuning_fork" title="Tuning fork">tuning fork</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowStuffWorks_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-HowStuffWorks-18">[18]</a></sup> It has a natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resonant_frequency" title="Resonant frequency">resonant frequency</a> at which it oscillates.  The resonant frequency of the receiver's tuned circuit is adjusted by the user to the frequency of the desired radio station; this is called "tuning".  The oscillating radio signal from the desired station causes the tuned circuit to <a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">resonate</a>, oscillate in sympathy, and it passes the signal on to the rest of the receiver.  Radio signals at other frequencies are blocked by the tuned circuit and not passed on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bandwidth">Bandwidth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Bandwidth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modulated_radio_signal_frequency_spectrum.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="437" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Modulated_radio_signal_frequency_spectrum.svg/220px-Modulated_radio_signal_frequency_spectrum.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Modulated_radio_signal_frequency_spectrum.svg/330px-Modulated_radio_signal_frequency_spectrum.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Modulated_radio_signal_frequency_spectrum.svg/440px-Modulated_radio_signal_frequency_spectrum.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modulated_radio_signal_frequency_spectrum.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">Frequency</a> spectrum of a typical modulated AM or FM radio signal.  It consists of a component <i>C</i> at the <a href="/wiki/Carrier_wave" title="Carrier wave">carrier wave</a> frequency <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f_{c}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>c</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f_{c}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f_{c}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d2b64a46c31800830b9fecc59b22812390e18c05" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.083ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> with the information (<a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">modulation</a>) contained in two narrow bands of frequencies called <a href="/wiki/Sideband" title="Sideband">sidebands</a> (<i>SB</i>) just above and below the carrier frequency.</div></div></div>
<p>A modulated radio wave, carrying an information signal, occupies a range of <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequencies</a>. The information (<a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">modulation</a>) in a radio signal is usually concentrated in narrow frequency bands called <a href="/wiki/Sideband" title="Sideband">sidebands</a> (<i>SB</i>) just above and below the <a href="/wiki/Carrier_wave" title="Carrier wave">carrier</a> frequency. The width in <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a> of the frequency range that the radio signal occupies, the highest frequency minus the lowest frequency, is called its <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">bandwidth</a> (<i>BW</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faruque_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-Faruque-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spectrum101_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spectrum101-24">[24]</a></sup> For any given <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">signal-to-noise ratio</a>, an amount of bandwidth can carry the same amount of information (<a href="/wiki/Data_signaling_rate" title="Data signaling rate">data rate</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_digit" title="Binary digit">bits</a> per second) regardless of where in the radio frequency spectrum it is located, so bandwidth is a measure of <a href="/wiki/Channel_capacity" title="Channel capacity">information-carrying capacity</a>. The bandwidth required by a radio transmission depends on the data rate of the information (modulation signal) being sent, and the <a href="/wiki/Spectral_efficiency" title="Spectral efficiency">spectral efficiency</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">modulation</a> method used; how much data it can transmit in each kilohertz of bandwidth.  Different types of information signals carried by radio have different data rates.  For example, a television (video) signal has a greater data rate than an <a href="/wiki/Audio_signal" title="Audio signal">audio signal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faruque_19-6"><a href="#cite_note-Faruque-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pogorel-Girard_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pogorel-Girard-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a>, the total range of radio frequencies that can be used for communication in a given area, is a limited resource.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spectrum101_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spectrum101-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellingson1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellingson1-3">[3]</a></sup> Each radio transmission occupies a portion of the total bandwidth available.  Radio bandwidth is regarded as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_good" title="Economic good">economic good</a> which has a monetary cost and is in increasing demand.  In some parts of the radio spectrum, the right to use a frequency band or even a single radio channel is bought and sold for millions of dollars.  So there is an incentive to employ technology to minimize the bandwidth used by radio services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pogorel-Girard_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pogorel-Girard-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>A slow transition from <a href="/wiki/Analog_signal" title="Analog signal">analog</a> to <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal" title="Digital signal">digital</a> radio transmission technologies began in the late 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>  Part of the reason for this is that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_modulation" title="Digital modulation">digital modulation</a> can often transmit more information (a greater data rate) in a given bandwidth than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analog_modulation" title="Analog modulation">analog modulation</a>, by using <a href="/wiki/Data_compression" title="Data compression">data compression</a> algorithms, which reduce redundancy in the data to be sent, and more efficient modulation.  Other reasons for the transition is that digital modulation has greater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noise_immunity" title="Noise immunity">noise immunity</a> than analog, <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal_processing" title="Digital signal processing">digital signal processing</a> chips have more power and flexibility than analog circuits, and a wide variety of types of information can be transmitted using the same digital modulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faruque_19-7"><a href="#cite_note-Faruque-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because it is a fixed resource which is in demand by an increasing number of users, the <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a> has become increasingly congested in recent decades, and the need to use it more effectively is driving many additional radio innovations such as <a href="/wiki/Trunked_radio_system" title="Trunked radio system">trunked radio systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spread_spectrum" title="Spread spectrum">spread spectrum</a> (ultra-wideband) transmission, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frequency_reuse" title="Frequency reuse">frequency reuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_spectrum_management" title="Dynamic spectrum management">dynamic spectrum management</a>, frequency pooling, and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_radio" title="Cognitive radio">cognitive radio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pogorel-Girard_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pogorel-Girard-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ITU_frequency_bands">ITU frequency bands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: ITU frequency bands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">ITU</a> arbitrarily divides the <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a> into 12 bands, each beginning at a wavelength which is a power of ten (10<sup>n</sup>) metres, with corresponding frequency of 3 times a power of ten, and each covering a decade of frequency or wavelength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellingson1_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ellingson1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITU2016_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITU2016-28">[28]</a></sup>  Each of these bands has a traditional name:
</p>

<p>It can be seen that the <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">bandwidth</a>, the range of frequencies, contained in each band is not equal but increases exponentially as the frequency increases; each band contains ten times the bandwidth of the preceding band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Radio_communication_service" title="Radio communication service">Radio communication service</a></div>
<p>The airwaves are a resource shared by many users.  Two radio transmitters in the same area that attempt to transmit on the same frequency will interfere with each other, causing garbled reception, so neither transmission may be received clearly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spectrum101_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Spectrum101-24">[24]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_frequency_interference" title="Radio frequency interference">Interference</a> with radio transmissions can not only have a large economic cost, it can be life-threatening (for example, in the case of interference with emergency communications or <a href="/wiki/Air_traffic_control" title="Air traffic control">air traffic control</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CISA_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-CISA-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mazar_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mazar-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>To prevent interference between different users, the emission of radio waves is strictly regulated by national laws, coordinated by an international body, the <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">International Telecommunication Union</a> (ITU), which allocates bands in the <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a> for different uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spectrum101_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Spectrum101-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellingson1_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ellingson1-3">[3]</a></sup> Radio transmitters must be licensed by governments, under a variety of license classes depending on use, and are restricted to certain frequencies and power levels.  In some classes, such as radio and television broadcasting stations, the transmitter is given a unique identifier consisting of a string of letters and numbers called a <i><a href="/wiki/Call_sign" title="Call sign">call sign</a></i>, which must be used in all transmissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITU1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITU1-32">[32]</a></sup> In order to adjust, maintain, or internally repair radiotelephone transmitters, individuals must hold a government license, such as the <a href="/wiki/General_radiotelephone_operator_license" title="General radiotelephone operator license">general radiotelephone operator license</a> in the US, obtained by taking a test demonstrating adequate technical and legal knowledge of safe radio operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCC2_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FCC2-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exceptions to the above rules allow the unlicensed operation by the public of low power short-range transmitters in consumer products such as cell phones, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cordless_phone" title="Cordless phone">cordless phones</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_device" title="Wireless device">wireless devices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walkie-talkie" title="Walkie-talkie">walkie-talkies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citizens_band_radio" title="Citizens band radio">citizens band radios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wireless_microphone" title="Wireless microphone">wireless microphones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garage_door_opener" title="Garage door opener">garage door openers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baby_monitor" title="Baby monitor">baby monitors</a>. In the US, these fall under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Part_15" title="Part 15">Part 15</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC) regulations.  Many of these devices use the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISM_band" title="ISM band">ISM bands</a>, a series of frequency bands throughout the radio spectrum reserved for unlicensed use.  Although they can be operated without a license, like all radio equipment these devices generally must be <a href="/wiki/Type_approval" title="Type approval">type-approved</a> before the sale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCC4_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FCC4-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Below are some of the most important uses of radio, organized by function.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcasting">Broadcasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Broadcasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting" title="Broadcasting">Broadcasting</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" station</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:112px;max-width:112px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:170px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FM_broadcasting_antenna_Willans_Hill.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="381" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/FM_broadcasting_antenna_Willans_Hill.jpg/110px-FM_broadcasting_antenna_Willans_Hill.jpg" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/FM_broadcasting_antenna_Willans_Hill.jpg/220px-FM_broadcasting_antenna_Willans_Hill.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">FM radio station</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:109px;max-width:109px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:170px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1023" data-file-width="641" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG/107px-Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG/161px-Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG/214px-Superturnstile_Tx_Muehlacker.JPG 2x" width="107"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Television station</div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Broadcasting antennas</div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Broadcasting" title="Broadcasting">Broadcasting</a> is the one-way transmission of information from a transmitter to receivers belonging to a public audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pizzi_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pizzi-35">[35]</a></sup> Since the radio waves become weaker with distance, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadcasting_station" title="Broadcasting station">broadcasting station</a> can only be received within a limited distance of its transmitter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witten_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witten-36">[36]</a></sup> Systems that broadcast from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communication_satellite" title="Communication satellite">satellites</a> can generally be received over an entire country or continent.  Older terrestrial radio and television are paid for by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_advertising" title="Commercial advertising">commercial advertising</a> or governments.  In subscription systems like <a href="/wiki/Satellite_television" title="Satellite television">satellite television</a> and <a href="/wiki/Satellite_radio" title="Satellite radio">satellite radio</a> the customer pays a monthly fee. In these systems, the radio signal is <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encrypted</a> and can only be decrypted by the receiver, which is controlled by the company and can be deactivated if there is no current subscription plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonsor_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonsor-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Broadcasting uses several parts of the radio spectrum, depending on the type of signals transmitted and the desired target audience. <a href="/wiki/Longwave" title="Longwave">Longwave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medium_wave" title="Medium wave">medium wave</a> signals can give reliable coverage of areas several hundred kilometers across, but have the more limited information-carrying capacity and so work best with audio signals (speech and music), and the sound quality can be degraded by <a href="/wiki/Radio_noise" title="Radio noise">radio noise</a> from natural and artificial sources.  The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave">shortwave</a> bands have a greater potential range but are more subject to interference by distant stations and varying atmospheric conditions that affect reception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gosling2_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gosling2-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffin_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griffin-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">very high frequency</a> band, greater than 30 megahertz, the Earth's atmosphere has less of an effect on the range of signals, and <a href="/wiki/Line-of-sight_propagation" title="Line-of-sight propagation">line-of-sight propagation</a> becomes the principal mode. These higher frequencies permit the great bandwidth required for television broadcasting.  Since natural and artificial noise sources are less present at these frequencies, high-quality audio transmission is possible, using <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">frequency modulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pizzi-Jones_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pizzi-Jones-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perez_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perez-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Audio:_Radio_broadcasting">Audio: Radio broadcasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Audio: Radio broadcasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Radio_broadcasting" title="Radio broadcasting">Radio broadcasting</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Radio_broadcasting" title="Radio broadcasting">Radio broadcasting</a> means transmission of <a href="/wiki/Audio_signal" title="Audio signal">audio</a> (sound) to <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver">radio receivers</a> belonging to a public audience.  Analog audio is the earliest form of radio broadcast. <a href="/wiki/AM_broadcasting" title="AM broadcasting">AM broadcasting</a> began around 1920. <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcasting" title="FM broadcasting">FM broadcasting</a> was introduced in the late 1930s with improved <a href="/wiki/Fidelity" title="Fidelity">fidelity</a>.  A broadcast radio receiver is called a <i>radio</i>.  Most radios can receive both AM and FM and are called AM/FM receivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>AM (<a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">amplitude modulation</a>) – in AM, the <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a> (strength) of the radio carrier wave is varied by the audio signal. <a href="/wiki/AM_broadcasting" title="AM broadcasting">AM broadcasting</a>, the oldest broadcasting technology, is allowed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AM_broadcast_band" title="AM broadcast band">AM broadcast bands</a>, between 148 and 283 kHz in the <a href="/wiki/Low_frequency" title="Low frequency">low frequency</a> (LF) band for longwave broadcasts and between 526 and 1706 kHz in the <a href="/wiki/Medium_frequency" title="Medium frequency">medium frequency</a> (MF) band for medium-wave broadcasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Because waves in these bands travel as <a href="/wiki/Ground_wave" title="Ground wave">ground waves</a> following the terrain, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AM_station" title="AM station">AM radio stations</a> can be received beyond the horizon at hundreds of miles distance, but AM has lower fidelity than FM.  Radiated power (<a href="/wiki/Effective_radiated_power" title="Effective radiated power">ERP</a>) of AM stations in the US is usually limited to a maximum of 10 kW, although a few (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clear-channel_stations" title="Clear-channel stations">clear-channel stations</a>) are allowed to transmit at 50 kW.  AM stations broadcast in <a href="/wiki/Monaural" title="Monaural">monaural</a> audio; <a href="/wiki/AM_stereo" title="AM stereo">AM stereo</a> broadcast standards exist in most countries, but the radio industry has failed to upgrade to them due to lack of demand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-44">[44]</a></sup>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Shortwave_listening" title="Shortwave listening">Shortwave broadcasting</a> – AM broadcasting is also allowed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave">shortwave</a> bands by legacy radio stations.  Since radio waves in these bands can travel intercontinental distances by reflecting off the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> using <a href="/wiki/Skywave" title="Skywave">skywave</a> or "skip" propagation, shortwave is used by international stations, broadcasting to other countries.<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KWNR_Continental_816R-5B_SN_247.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/KWNR_Continental_816R-5B_SN_247.jpg/220px-KWNR_Continental_816R-5B_SN_247.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/KWNR_Continental_816R-5B_SN_247.jpg/330px-KWNR_Continental_816R-5B_SN_247.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/KWNR_Continental_816R-5B_SN_247.jpg/440px-KWNR_Continental_816R-5B_SN_247.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KWNR_Continental_816R-5B_SN_247.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>FM broadcast transmitter of radio station <a href="/wiki/KWNR" title="KWNR">KWNR</a>, Las Vegas which transmits on 95.5 MHz with a power of 35 kW</div></div></div><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berg1_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berg1-45">[45]</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>FM (<a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">frequency modulation</a>) – in FM the <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> of the radio carrier signal is varied slightly by the audio signal. <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcasting" title="FM broadcasting">FM broadcasting</a> is permitted in the <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcast_band" title="FM broadcast band">FM broadcast bands</a> between about 65 and 108 MHz in the <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">very high frequency</a> (VHF) range.  Radio waves in this band travel by <a href="/wiki/Line-of-sight_propagation" title="Line-of-sight propagation">line-of-sight</a> so FM reception is limited by the  visual <a href="/wiki/Horizon" title="Horizon">horizon</a> to about 30–40 mi (48–64 km), and can be blocked by hills.  However it is less susceptible to interference from <a href="/wiki/Radio_noise" title="Radio noise">radio noise</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_frequency_interference" title="Radio frequency interference">RFI</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sferics" title="Sferics">sferics</a>, static) and has higher <a href="/wiki/Fidelity" title="Fidelity">fidelity</a>; better <a href="/wiki/Frequency_response" title="Frequency response">frequency response</a> and less <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_distortion" title="Audio distortion">audio distortion</a>, than AM. In the US, radiated power (<a href="/wiki/Effective_radiated_power" title="Effective radiated power">ERP</a>) of FM stations varies from 6 to 100 kW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sterling-Kieth_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sterling-Kieth-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Digital_radio" title="Digital radio">Digital radio</a> involves a variety of standards and technologies for broadcasting digital radio signals over the air. Some systems, such as <a href="/wiki/HD_Radio" title="HD Radio">HD Radio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Digital_Radio_Mondiale" title="Digital Radio Mondiale">DRM</a>, operate in the same wavebands as analog broadcasts either as a replacement for analog stations or a complementary service. Others, such as <a href="/wiki/Digital_Audio_Broadcasting" title="Digital Audio Broadcasting">DAB/DAB+</a> and <a href="/wiki/ISDB" title="ISDB">ISDB_Tsb</a>, operate in wavebands traditionally used for television or satellite services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Digital_Audio_Broadcasting" title="Digital Audio Broadcasting">Digital Audio Broadcasting</a> (DAB) debuted in some countries in 1998. It transmits audio as a <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal" title="Digital signal">digital signal</a> rather than an <a href="/wiki/Analog_signal" title="Analog signal">analog signal</a> as AM and FM do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-48">[48]</a></sup> DAB has the potential to provide higher quality sound than FM (although many stations do not choose to transmit at such high quality), has greater immunity to <a href="/wiki/Radio_noise" title="Radio noise">radio noise</a> and interference, makes better use of scarce <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a> bandwidth and provides advanced user features such as <a href="/wiki/Electronic_program_guide" title="Electronic program guide">electronic program guides</a>. Its disadvantage is that it is incompatible with previous radios so that a new DAB receiver must be purchased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoeg-Lauterbach_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoeg-Lauterbach-49">[49]</a></sup> Several nations have set dates to switch off analog FM networks in favor of DAB/DAB+, notably Norway in 2017<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and Switzerland in 2024.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>A single DAB station transmits a <span class="hMeasure frequency"><span class="num">1,500</span> <span class="unit">kHz</span></span> bandwidth signal that carries from 9 to 12 channels of digital audio modulated by <a href="/wiki/Orthogonal_frequency-division_multiplexing" title="Orthogonal frequency-division multiplexing">OFDM</a> from which the listener can choose.  Broadcasters can transmit a channel at a range of different <a href="/wiki/Bit_rate" title="Bit rate">bit rates</a>, so different channels can have different audio quality.  In different countries DAB stations broadcast in either <a href="/wiki/Band_III" title="Band III">Band III</a> (174–240 MHz) or <a href="/wiki/L_band" title="L band">L band</a> (1.452–1.492 GHz) in the UHF range, so like FM reception is limited by the visual horizon to about 40 miles (64 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoeg-Lauterbach_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoeg-Lauterbach-49">[49]</a></sup></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/HD_Radio" title="HD Radio">HD Radio</a> is an alternative digital radio standard widely implemented in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/In-band_on-channel" title="In-band on-channel">In-band on-channel</a> technology, HD Radio broadcasts a digital signal in a subcarrier of a station's analog FM or AM signal. Stations are able to <a href="/wiki/Multicast" title="Multicast">multicast</a> more than one audio signal in the subcarrier, supporting the transmission of multiple audio services at varying bitrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The digital signal is transmitted using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OFDM" title="OFDM">OFDM</a> with the HDC (<a href="/wiki/High-Definition_Coding" title="High-Definition Coding">High-Definition Coding</a>) proprietary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_compression_format" title="Audio compression format">audio compression format</a>. HDC is based upon, but incompatible with, <a href="/wiki/MPEG-4" title="MPEG-4">MPEG-4</a> standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HE-AAC" title="HE-AAC">HE-AAC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> It uses a <a href="/wiki/Modified_discrete_cosine_transform" title="Modified discrete cosine transform">modified discrete cosine transform</a> (MDCT) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_data_compression" title="Audio data compression">audio data compression</a> algorithm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Digital_Radio_Mondiale" title="Digital Radio Mondiale">Digital Radio Mondiale</a> (DRM) is a competing digital terrestrial radio standard developed mainly by broadcasters as a higher <a href="/wiki/Spectral_efficiency" title="Spectral efficiency">spectral efficiency</a> replacement for legacy AM and FM broadcasting.  <i>Mondiale</i> means "worldwide" in French and Italian, and DRM, developed in 2001, is currently supported by 23 countries and has been adopted by some European and Eastern broadcasters beginning in 2003. The DRM30 mode uses the AM broadcast bands below 30 MHz and is intended as a replacement for AM and shortwave broadcasting, and the DRM+ mode uses <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">VHF</a> frequencies centered on the FM broadcast band and is intended as a replacement for FM broadcasting.  It is incompatible with existing radio receivers and requires listeners to purchase a new DRM receiver. The modulation used is a form of <a href="/wiki/Orthogonal_frequency-division_multiplexing" title="Orthogonal frequency-division multiplexing">OFDM</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COFDM" title="COFDM">COFDM</a> in which up to 4 carriers are transmitted in a channel formerly occupied by a single AM or FM signal, modulated by <a href="/wiki/Quadrature_amplitude_modulation" title="Quadrature amplitude modulation">quadrature amplitude modulation</a> (QAM).  The DRM system is designed to be as compatible as possible with existing AM and FM radio transmitters, so much of the equipment in existing radio stations will not have to be replaced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berg1_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berg1-45">[45]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Satellite_radio" title="Satellite radio">Satellite radio</a> is a subscription radio service that broadcasts CD quality <a href="/wiki/Digital_audio" title="Digital audio">digital audio</a> direct to subscribers' receivers using a <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downlink" title="Downlink">downlink</a> signal from a <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">direct broadcast communication satellite</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geostationary" title="Geostationary">geostationary</a> orbit 22,000 miles (35,000 km) above the Earth.  It is mostly intended for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_radio" title="Car radio">car radios</a> in vehicles.  Satellite radio uses the 2.3 GHz <a href="/wiki/S_band" title="S band">S band</a> in North America, in other parts of the world, it uses the 1.4 GHz <a href="/wiki/L_band" title="L band">L band</a> allocated for DAB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csgnetwork.com_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-csgnetwork.com-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kbonsor_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-kbonsor-59">[59]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sony_KDL-40V2500_20061107.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1353" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Sony_KDL-40V2500_20061107.jpg/150px-Sony_KDL-40V2500_20061107.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Sony_KDL-40V2500_20061107.jpg/225px-Sony_KDL-40V2500_20061107.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Sony_KDL-40V2500_20061107.jpg/300px-Sony_KDL-40V2500_20061107.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sony_KDL-40V2500_20061107.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Television receiver</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Video:_Television_broadcasting">Video: Television broadcasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Video: Television broadcasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Television_broadcasting" title="Television broadcasting">Television broadcasting</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Television_broadcasting" title="Television broadcasting">Television broadcasting</a> is the transmission of moving images by radio, which consist of sequences of still images, which are displayed on a screen on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_receiver" title="Television receiver">television receiver</a> (a "television" or TV) along with a synchronized audio (sound) channel. Television (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_signal" title="Video signal">video</a>) signals occupy a wider <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">bandwidth</a> than broadcast radio (<a href="/wiki/Audio_signal" title="Audio signal">audio</a>) signals.  <a href="/wiki/Analog_television" title="Analog television">Analog television</a>, the original television technology, required 6 MHz, so the television frequency bands are divided into 6 MHz channels, now called "RF channels".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enticknap_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enticknap-60">[60]</a></sup>  The current television standard, introduced beginning in 2006, is a digital format called <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">high-definition television</a> (HDTV), which transmits pictures at higher resolution, typically 1080 <a href="/wiki/Pixel" title="Pixel">pixels</a> high by 1920 pixels wide, at a rate of 25 or 30 frames per second.  <a href="/wiki/Digital_television" title="Digital television">Digital television</a> (DTV) transmission systems, which replaced older analog television in a <a href="/wiki/Digital_television_transition" title="Digital television transition">transition</a> beginning in 2006, use <a href="/wiki/Image_compression" title="Image compression">image compression</a> and high-efficiency digital modulation such as <a href="/wiki/Orthogonal_frequency-division_multiplexing" title="Orthogonal frequency-division multiplexing">OFDM</a> and <a href="/wiki/8VSB" title="8VSB">8VSB</a> to transmit HDTV video within a smaller bandwidth than the old analog channels, saving scarce <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a> space.  Therefore, each of the 6 MHz analog RF channels now carries up to 7 DTV channels – these are called "virtual channels". Digital television receivers have different behavior in the presence of poor reception or noise than analog television, called the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_cliff" title="Digital cliff">digital cliff</a>" effect.  Unlike analog television, in which increasingly poor reception causes the picture quality to gradually degrade, in digital television picture quality is not affected by poor reception until, at a certain point, the receiver stops working and the screen goes black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starks_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starks-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brice_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brice-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_television" title="Terrestrial television">Terrestrial television</a>, <i>over-the-air (OTA) television</i>, or <i>broadcast television</i> – the oldest television technology, is the transmission of television signals from land-based <a href="/wiki/Television_station" title="Television station">television stations</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_receiver" title="Television receiver">television receivers</a> (called <i>televisions</i> or TVs) in viewer's homes.  Terrestrial television broadcasting uses the bands 41 – 88 MHz (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VHF" title="VHF">VHF</a> low band or <a href="/wiki/Band_I" title="Band I">Band I</a>, carrying RF channels 1–6), 174 – 240 MHz, (VHF high band or <a href="/wiki/Band_III" title="Band III">Band III</a>; carrying RF channels 7–13), and 470 – 614 MHz (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UHF" title="UHF">UHF</a> <a href="/wiki/Band_IV" title="Band IV">Band IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Band_V" title="Band V">Band V</a>; carrying RF channels 14 and up).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAB1_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAB1-63">[63]</a></sup>  The exact frequency boundaries vary in different countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lundstrom_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lundstrom-64">[64]</a></sup>  Propagation is by <a href="/wiki/Line-of-sight_propagation" title="Line-of-sight propagation">line-of-sight</a>, so reception is limited by the visual horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ingram_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ingram-65">[65]</a></sup>  In the US, the <a href="/wiki/Effective_radiated_power" title="Effective radiated power">effective radiated power</a> (ERP) of television transmitters is regulated according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HAAT" title="HAAT">height above average terrain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProStar_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ProStar-66">[66]</a></sup> Viewers closer to the television transmitter can use a simple "rabbit ears" <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipole antenna</a> on top of the TV, but viewers in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fringe_reception" title="Fringe reception">fringe reception</a> areas typically require an outdoor antenna mounted on the roof to get adequate reception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ingram_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ingram-65">[65]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SuperDISH121.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="243" data-file-width="208" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/SuperDISH121.jpg/110px-SuperDISH121.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/SuperDISH121.jpg/165px-SuperDISH121.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/SuperDISH121.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SuperDISH121.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Satellite_dish" title="Satellite dish">Satellite television dish</a> on a residence</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Satellite_television" title="Satellite television">Satellite television</a> – a <a href="/wiki/Set-top_box" title="Set-top box">set-top box</a> which receives subscription <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct-broadcast_satellite_television" title="Direct-broadcast satellite television">direct-broadcast satellite television</a>, and displays it on an ordinary <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_broadcast_satellite" title="Direct broadcast satellite">direct broadcast satellite</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geostationary" title="Geostationary">geostationary</a> orbit 22,200 miles (35,700 km) above the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> transmits many channels (up to 900) modulated on a 12.2 to 12.7 GHz <a href="/wiki/Ku_band" title="Ku band">K<sub>u</sub> band</a> <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> downlink signal to a rooftop <a href="/wiki/Satellite_dish" title="Satellite dish">satellite dish</a> antenna on the subscriber's residence.  The microwave signal is converted to a lower <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_frequency" title="Intermediate frequency">intermediate frequency</a> at the dish and conducted into the building by a coaxial cable to a <a href="/wiki/Set-top_box" title="Set-top box">set-top box</a> connected to the subscriber's TV, where it is demodulated and displayed. The subscriber pays a monthly fee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benoit_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benoit-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Long-68">[68]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Time">Time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Government <a href="/wiki/Standard_frequency_and_time_signal_service" title="Standard frequency and time signal service">standard frequency and time signal services</a> operate time radio stations which continuously broadcast extremely accurate time signals produced by <a href="/wiki/Atomic_clock" title="Atomic clock">atomic clocks</a>, as a reference to synchronize other clocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weik_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weik-69">[69]</a></sup>  Examples are <a href="/wiki/BPC_(time_signal)" title="BPC (time signal)">BPC</a>, <a href="/wiki/DCF77" title="DCF77">DCF77</a>, <a href="/wiki/JJY" title="JJY">JJY</a>, <a href="/wiki/Time_from_NPL_(MSF)" title="Time from NPL (MSF)">MSF</a>, <a href="/wiki/RTZ_(radio_station)" title="RTZ (radio station)">RTZ</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TDF_time_signal" title="TDF time signal">TDF</a>, <a href="/wiki/WWV_(radio_station)" title="WWV (radio station)">WWV</a>, and <a href="/wiki/YVTO" title="YVTO">YVTO</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RadioNavAids_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-RadioNavAids-70">[70]</a></sup>  One use is in <a href="/wiki/Radio_clock" title="Radio clock">radio clocks</a> and watches, which include an automated receiver that periodically (usually weekly) receives and decodes the time signal and resets the watch's internal <a href="/wiki/Quartz_clock" title="Quartz clock">quartz clock</a> to the correct time, thus allowing a small watch or desk clock to have the same accuracy as an atomic clock.  Government time stations are declining in number because <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_positioning_system" title="Global positioning system">GPS</a> satellites and the Internet <a href="/wiki/Network_Time_Protocol" title="Network Time Protocol">Network Time Protocol</a> (NTP) provide equally accurate time standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Two-way_voice_communication">Two-way voice communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Two-way voice communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Two-way_radio" title="Two-way radio">Two-way radio</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:256px;max-width:256px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HA0478-006_(6011470974).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4354" data-file-width="4014" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/HA0478-006_%286011470974%29.jpg/140px-HA0478-006_%286011470974%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/HA0478-006_%286011470974%29.jpg/210px-HA0478-006_%286011470974%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/HA0478-006_%286011470974%29.jpg/280px-HA0478-006_%286011470974%29.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:110px;max-width:110px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cell-Tower.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2980" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Cell-Tower.jpg/108px-Cell-Tower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Cell-Tower.jpg/162px-Cell-Tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Cell-Tower.jpg/216px-Cell-Tower.jpg 2x" width="108"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><i>(left)</i> Cellphone from the early 21st century. <i>(right)</i> Cellular phone tower shared by antennas belonging to 3 different networks.</div></div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Two-way_radio" title="Two-way radio">two-way radio</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Audio_signal" title="Audio signal">audio</a> <a href="/wiki/Transceiver" title="Transceiver">transceiver</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver">receiver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transmitter" title="Transmitter">transmitter</a> in the same device, used for bidirectional person-to-person voice communication with other users with similar radios. An older term for this mode of communication is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotelephony" title="Radiotelephony">radiotelephony</a></i>.  The radio link may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-duplex" title="Half-duplex">half-duplex</a>, as in a <a href="/wiki/Walkie-talkie" title="Walkie-talkie">walkie-talkie</a>, using a single radio channel in which only one radio can transmit at a time, so different users take turns talking, pressing a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Push_to_talk" title="Push to talk">push to talk</a>" button on their radio which switches off the receiver and switches on the transmitter.  Or the radio link may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full_duplex" title="Full duplex">full duplex</a>, a bidirectional link using two radio channels so both people can talk at the same time, as in a cell phone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frenzel_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frenzel-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_phone" title="Cell phone">Cell phone</a> – a portable wireless <a href="/wiki/Telephone" title="Telephone">telephone</a> that is connected to the <a href="/wiki/Public_switched_telephone_network" title="Public switched telephone network">telephone network</a> by radio signals exchanged with a local antenna at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cellular_base_station" title="Cellular base station">cellular base station</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_tower" title="Cell tower">cell tower</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowCellPhonesWork_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-HowCellPhonesWork-73">[73]</a></sup> The service area covered by the provider is divided into small geographical areas called "cells", each served by a separate base station antenna and multichannel <a href="/wiki/Transceiver" title="Transceiver">transceiver</a>.  All the cell phones in a cell communicate with this antenna on separate frequency channels, assigned from a common pool of frequencies. The purpose of cellular organization is to conserve radio bandwidth by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frequency_reuse" title="Frequency reuse">frequency reuse</a>.  Low power transmitters are used so the radio waves used in a cell do not travel far beyond the cell, allowing the same frequencies to be reused in geographically separated cells.  When a user carrying a cellphone crosses from one cell to another, his phone is automatically "handed off" seamlessly to the new antenna and assigned new frequencies.  Cellphones have a highly automated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full_duplex" title="Full duplex">full duplex</a> digital <a href="/wiki/Transceiver" title="Transceiver">transceiver</a> using <a href="/wiki/Orthogonal_frequency-division_multiplexing" title="Orthogonal frequency-division multiplexing">OFDM</a> modulation using two digital radio channels, each carrying one direction of the bidirectional conversation, as well as a control channel that handles dialing calls and "handing off" the phone to another cell tower.  Older <a href="/wiki/2G" title="2G">2G</a>, <a href="/wiki/3G" title="3G">3G</a>, and <a href="/wiki/4G" title="4G">4G</a> networks use frequencies in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultrahigh_frequency" title="Ultrahigh frequency">UHF</a> and low microwave range, between 700 MHz and 3 GHz.  The cell phone transmitter adjusts its power output to use the minimum power necessary to communicate with the cell tower; 0.6 W when near the tower, up to 3 W when farther away.  Cell tower channel transmitter power is 50 W.  Current generation phones, called <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>, have many functions besides making telephone calls, and therefore have several other radio transmitters and receivers that connect them with other networks: usually a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_modem" title="Wireless modem">Wi-Fi modem</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">Bluetooth</a> modem, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS_receiver" title="GPS receiver">GPS receiver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawson_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zander_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zander-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/5G" title="5G">5G cellular network</a> – next-generation cellular networks which began deployment in 2019.  Their major advantage is much higher <a href="/wiki/Data_signaling_rate" title="Data signaling rate">data rates</a> than previous cellular networks, up to 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bits_per_second" title="Bits per second">Gbps</a>; 100 times faster than the previous cellular technology, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4G_LTE" title="4G LTE">4G LTE</a>. The higher data rates are achieved partly by using higher frequency radio waves, in the higher microwave band 3–6 GHz, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millimeter_wave" title="Millimeter wave">millimeter wave</a> band, around 28 and 39 GHz.  Since these frequencies have a shorter range than previous cellphone bands, the cells will be smaller than the cells in previous cellular networks which could be many miles across.
